There arc numerous other minor changes in the pattcm of drapery, of curls on the forchcad, and in the dressing of the hair (in the plaster, for instance, the plaits are wound around the hair-scarf and tied in a bow above the sitter's left eye). Confirmation of the suspicion that Chinard's talents were those of a modeller rather than a carver is provided by the prominent signature on the marble bust in Lyons (`Chinard de Lyon') which is a reproduction of the script with which he incised his clay (hc used numerous fomulac+`Chinard fct. I.ami (op. cit.) lists a number of versions of the portrait in terracotta and bronze which may be carly; but the great majority of versions are of marble, and of inferior quality, and there is no reason to suppose that they are more than a hundred years old. The Ashmolcan's version is one of twenty or so that I have noted in the last two years, all in Britain. In these marbles the shawl is often punched lightly to suggest a floral pattern, and there is a distinct text`ml contrast with the flesh (this may be seen in the Ashmolean's version, and also, in a more marked degrcc, in the version sold Phiuip's, I.ondon, 26 November 1985, lot 93, and in that in the City Art Gaucry and Museum, Bristol (33-1947)). 358 at Sotheby's, London, 12 June 1987, was an example of this). The curls on the forehead tend to be very thin and schematic and there is never any undercutting of the crowning ringlets to compare with that in the Lyons and Leningrad marbles. An unhappy aspect of the Ashmolcan version, which is not typical of these latter marble versions, is the absence of a soclc plinth for the falling drapery to fold over. @@PROCESS@@
Copper and bronze, fire-gilt, with pearls, gold settings, gold `palllons', and enamels-`en plein' for the grisaillc scenes, `cn basse tallle', translucent grccn and red, for lcavcs and flower-heads. The dlding of the bronze has tamishcd in a few areas especiauy on the |ions' heads of the tripods and the detachable nozzles. Most of the gilding sccms to have been matt but thcrc is a highly burnished rim above-nd contrasting with-the minutely punched laurel leaf and berry ornament on the base. Some of the smaller ornament is machined: for instance, the plaited mouldings of the trays and nozzles. On one candlestick the lowest band of blue enamel is cracked in several places and two small pieces are missing. On the other there is a slight crackle on the enamel of the nozzle. It seems likely that some ornament (probably a small flame) was originally placed above the tiny urns inside the tripod legs: the top of ca.ch is closed with a small hole in the centre of the cover. `M 185/2' is painted in black on the Slt copper plates covering the undersides of the bases. Bequeathed by J. F. Mallctt who died 7January 1947. Received in the Museum during the last week of May 1947.185/2 in the inventory of his bequest. Valued by Mallett at £260. Bought by him at the sale of the property of Mrs George I.ockett deceased of 58 Princes Gate, I.ondon, at Christie's, I.ondon,11 June 1942, lot 139. Previous to that in the collection of Sir Philip Sassoon and I.ady Rocksavage, sold Christie's, I.ondon, 26-7 November 1919 (for
950 guincas), and said to have been in the collection of Baron Gustave de Rothschild. George Lockctt's collection (sold after his widow's death) was famous for its silver, its miniatures, its snuff-boxes, and scent bottles. These candlesticks wcrc repeatedly stated to have been the property of Marie-Antoinette and to have come from the Trianon. Taken out of `storc' in the room long occupied by Ian I.owe and placed on display with the Sevres porcelain in May 1989. Mallett justly observed that `evcry detail of the design' of these candlestands `exhibits the greatest delicacy and highest possible finish'. The cameo rescrvcs and the colouring, especiany the dominant deep blue, arc inspired by, and perhaps even intended to hamonizc with, larger French ceramic pieces (Mallctt, in common with the auction catalogucs, mistakenly described the trays as `of blue Sevres porcelain'). It was in the 1780s and early 1790s, when these must have been made, that the most remarkable collaborations between craftsmen in porcelain and in ormolu took place-in the candelabra of American Independence signed by Thomirc in 1784, for instance, with their Sevrcs cameos. But if there is a relationship with porcelain then there is also one with precious metal work. The rows of tiny gold-mounted green, white, and turquoise enamel beads emulating semi- Precious stones, combined with real pearls; the gold fronds on the blue enamel ground and combined with translucent green enamel leaves and red enamel flowers on the white enamel ground arc typical of taste of Parisian high society under Louis XVI more commonly manifested in patch and snuff-boxes or court sword hilts than in candlestands. The enamel grisalles, in oval reserves (with black backgrounds) on the bases and on the blue vases within the tripods, are executed in subtle combinations of blue-grey and pale brown-grey. They differ on the two candlestands. One scene on one of the vases seems to represent Leda enjoyed by the swan but in most the action cannot be identified or intcxpreted-thcre is a scatcd nymph holding a distaff, accompanied by Cupid; another crowning Cupid with flowers, another reading by an altar, another tending an altar, and so on. On the nozzles of both candlcstands in lozenge rcscrves arc a fleur-dc- lis, a single castle, and a rampant lion crowned for France, Castile, and Le6n. The lozenges suggest a female owner and the heraldry would suit the Spanish Bourbons, but hardly Maric-Antoincttc to whom it has bccn claimed that they belonged. Michael Maclagan has pointed out to mc that any ruler of spain after Philip V (d.1746) would use thrcc flcur-de- lis for Anjou and suspects that the use is decorative. John Whitehead notes that thcrc are comparable candlesticks at Tatton Park, Cheshire (National Trust) and is `convinccd that they were part of clock gamitures sold by Godon to the Spanish Court' (letter of 15 March 1991 to the kccpcr). Mallctt followed the auction catalogue in describing these candlestands as enamelled `in the manner of Cottcau', that is in the manner of Jean Coteau who is known to have worked for the Sevres factory between 1780 and 1782 and to have cxcclled in the use of gold `paillons'; the `paillons' in thcsc candlestands arc similar to those on a gold box signed by him which also has a deep violet-blue emmel ground and `Pompeian' scenes in grisaillc (A. K. Snowman, Edy4fec„£4 Century Gold Bores Of. Europe (.hondon, r966), 57 a;nd pl. 422). Coteau is also known to have perfected cnamcl jewellery as excmplificd by the beads found hcrc. Good examples of such work are the `gobelet citron' in the Royal Couection (S. Eriksen and G. de Bellalgue, SforGf Force/¢¢.» (I.ondon, 1987), 139, pl. P), the egg box in the Musec dcs Art D€coratifs, Paris (Gr. 294), which is of a type Cotcau was documcnted as making together with the Sldcr I.e Guay (ibid., fig. 232, p. 201), and the lyre clock cases of omolu and sivres porcelain which he signed in 1 786 and I 787 (Musec National Ceramiquc dc Sevrcs, Inv. 21649, Mus€c du I.ouvre, OA. R483-fig. 264, p. 212, of M. Brunet and T. Prfaud, Sforcf.. DGf o"j¢.„Gf d „oj/.o#rf (Fribourg, 1978)). The grisaille decoration on the other hand recalls those miniatures often found on gold boxes with similar jewcllcd enamel frames signed by Jacques-Joseph dc Gault or Degault (he used both styles) and dated in the 1780s-for which see S. Grandfean. Les Tabati6res dtt Mtt§6e dtt I:ourre (Paris, 1981), cat. nos. 55, 90,103,172, 264, 284. The folds of drapery over the abdomen in low relief are clearer than in the Ashmolcan version, the leaves on the ground more crisply defined and more deeply undercut, the bark more sharply distinguished; there is no circular clasp at the shoulder, the navel is indicated nearer to the belt, and the child's head is at a different anale. Thcrc is no evidence to suggest that Coyscvox or his contcmporarics would have ever made large terracotta figures of this kind; they would, doubtless, have made clay figures on such a scale but wc do not know that they had the facilities for firing them nor that thcrc was demand for work of this sort. Experiments with the production of large fired figures are associated with the late cightcenth century in both England and France (especially in connection with the fashion for decorative lamp-bearing statues) but the terracotta replicas of Coysevox's Dry#d was more tikely to have been made in the last quarter of the nineteenth century when the sculpture of Coysevox was so kccnly admired and when the high-quahity reproduction of large terracotta figures was a large industry in Paris. The Dry#d was certainly rcproduccd in other media in this period. It fomed a pendant to Coysevox's I/a/¢, cast by Barbcdienne (a pair signed `F. His first appearance in Daumier's lithographs was in the Cho".p¢7?. of 11 October in that year and he fcatured frequently in Daumier's contributions to that journal during the summer of the following year as the type of the shabby, swaggering, militarist bully who was to make possible the coap d'G'£#f of I.ouis Napoleon on 2 Dcccmbcr 1851 which dcstroycd the Republican government which Daumier supported. Hc carries a knotted cudgel, wears hussar trousers, and sports the moustache and goatcc favoured by the future emperor. It may be assumed that the oriSnal clay model was made in 1851 when the figure increasindy engaged the artist's imagivation. Hc created it perhaps partly as a stimulant to the plasticity of his drawings, perhaps also bccausc the demonic vitality of the invention demanded more complete realzation than was possible in two dimensions. Given the figure's wiry character, and the thin clcmcnts of cudgel and linbs especially, the clay must have been worked on an armature and would therefore have been inpossible to fire, whilst its chances of survival unfircd would have been far less good than was the case with the grotesque busts which were otherwise Daumicr's chief sculptural production. Not surprisingiv therefore Daumier had the clay cast in plaster. This was done by his friend Victor Geoffroy-Dechaune almost certainly using a waste mould. The oriSnal plaster which was then cast is the one in the Albright-Knox Museum, Buffalo. This plaster is said to have been concealed during the Second Empire. After Daumicr's death his widow authorized a sman bronze edition, which was cast, using the lost-wax process, by the Siot-Decauville foundry between 1888 and 1892. The Ashmolean bronze is from this edition. The exact size of the edition is not known and there may have been two series. Some bronzcs are numbcrcd out of 10 (e.g. `7/10' sold Christie's, London, 2 May 1969, lot 30) others are simply numbered (no. 3, in the concction of Paul Rene Roussel before 1928, was sold Christie's, Iondon, 31 March 1987, lot 52; no.17 is in the Roscnwald Collection, National Gancry of Art, Washington; no. 20 is in the Ny Carlsbcrg Glyptotck, Copcnhagcn). One edition may have been made for the State, the other on the initiative of Madame Daumier. A later edition of about twenty was made from the same plaster model, by now damaged in some minor details, by Alexis Rudicr's foundry in 1925. A third edition of fifteen in about 1959ndo was made by the Valsuani foundry, using a plaster cast (in a private collection in Italy) apparently made from the origival plaster at sometime before the Rudicr edition. There are also aftercasts from the bronzes of these three editions and rephicas in various synthetic materials. The history of ire statuette, in all its metamorphoses, has been traced with great ingenuity and meticulousness by J. L. Wasscrman, J. M. Lukach, and A. Beale in D¢#owG.Gr Sc#/P£#rG.. These busts are closely related to a pair of full-length bronze figures with heads of similar size but diffcrcntly dressed (Musec du Ilouvre, MR 3256 and 3257) and with the larger figures possibly origivany mounted as andirons, sold by the descendants of the Marquis de Contades at Christie's, Monaco, 7 December 1985, lot 27 (illustrated), and acquired for the I.ouvre. In these latter bronzes, which are of outstanding quality, the nude king is given the attributes of Jupiter and the scanuly clad queen those of Juno. Dupie was Prieur's son-in-law and the two men arc known to have collaborated. The depiction of king and queen is close to that on Duprfe's medals, but the large full-length figure of the king retains Prieur's name chiselled on its base and both it and its companion are very similar in style to documented bronzes by Prieur. Also, several sets of busts of the king and queen arc listed in the posthumous inventory of Prieur's possessions (M. I,any, `L'Invcntairc dc Barthelemy Pricur', Bulletin historique et litt6raire de la Socict6 d'histoire du Protestantismefrangais(\949),52A). Duprf and Prieur were personally conccrncd in the casting of their works. This bust, like the other versions and the related figures, is a superlative example of lost-wax casting: all the detal in hair and eyebrows and in the crinkles of the soft drapery at the neck is reproduced from the wax model. The sculptor must have had two moulds from which he cast the wax models lost in each bronze casting: one for the head and one for the bust; and although the assembled wax model would bc reworked prior to the casting the junction is clearly apparent on the interior whcrc thcrc is a line of flashing in the bronze. This is not the case with all other versions of the bust. For example, the version in the Christic's sale, 23 June 1988 (listed above), has no flashing in the interior. In this version also some details differ shightly from the Ashmolean version-above all, the epaulette straps and the border at collar and neck are fluted. The socle is identical to that of the Ashmolean version and rilded. It should be noted that the Ashmolean bust is identical with that in the Daniels Collection, although said in the catalogue (cited above) to be different in the amour. The Danicls version is now in the County Museum of Art, I.os Angcles (M.81.22. I). Weihrauch made the sensible suggestion (E#„oP¢3.fcife Bro#zcJf¢f"c#¢" (Brunswick,1967), 388, 395) that the busts may have been made around 1600, the date of the marriage between Henri IV and Marie dc Medicis. In any case there is no earlier known example of editions of small bronze busts being made of monarchs and the idea may have come from the practice of medallists. Puget's marble statue of MG./a (the wrestling champion who trapped his hands in a trcc hc was attempting to wrench asunder and was, in consequence, unable to protect himself against attack by savage beasts) was a subject selected by Pugct himself in response to a request in 1670 from Colbert to carve a group for the King. It was begun in 1672, signed in 1682, and placed in the park at Versalllcs in 1683. It is now in the Mus6c du Louvre (MR. 2075). The Ashmolean's bronze is not an exact reproduction of the marble. The drapery, for example, is cut off in the bronze whereas in the marble it overlaps the base and is properly terminated. In the marble the chisellcd surface of the rock and twisted structure of the bark are also more apparent. It seems possible that the bronze reproduces a preparatory model by Puget like the splendid one in a private collection in Paris which is of similar size (Klaus Herding, P¢.c77G P#gGf (Berlin, 1970), Cat. 38A, p. 173, and pl. 179). The impossible date of 1622 may be explained as follows. If the date 1672 was inscribed funtly on a terracotta model from which casts were taken to make the wax model, then, when this wax was prepared for investment, the details would have been sharpened and the date misread and altered to read 1622. Such an error would have been more likely, of course, some years after Puget's death. Ion Robertson describing the bronze in the A"„"a/ Rcporf fo fbc V¢.fG.forJ for 1960, p. 48, claimed that the signature `has every appearance of being contemporary and genuine' whereas the date is `certainly a later addition'. In fact they have every appearance of being made at the same time by the same hand and both in the model not in the metal. There is also no reason to suppose that Puget was personally involved in making any bronze reproductions of his work. RIaus Herding sent to the Museum on 6 September 1971 photographs of a bronze of nearly the sanie size and similar style also dated 1622 which had been sold at Versailles in 1966 noted as cast type (c) in his monograph on Puget (op. cit. 173). This bronze does not merit the dismissive description of `Rleinbronze mindcrcr Qualitat'-nor certainly does the Ashmolean's bronze. The latter was erront,ously included by Herding in his catalogue as cast type (b) (op. cit. 172-3), that is as a bronze 98 cms. high made by I.ouis Desprcz (1799-1870) in 1835, examples of which are to be found at the museums in Aix-en-Provence, Dole, I.c Puy, Lyons, and Rouen. These sand-casts by Desprcz, like the smaller bronzes (64.3 cms. high) in the Walters Art Gallery, Baltimore (54.729), and in the National Ganery, Washington (Andrew Mcllon Fund 1985.51. I), to cite two examples with very different patinas (but of identical size and mode of manufacture), classified by Herding as type (d), are clearly made ¢¢Gr the finished marble. But this was not the case with the Ashmolean's bronze. Workshops of Andre-Antoine RAVRIO (1759-1814) or of Pierre-Philippe THOMIRE (1751-1843).

309. Head of Honor6 de Balzac (1799-1850)
17.95 cms. (height of bronze); 15.3 cms. (height, length, and width of cube base)
@@PROCESS@@
Bronze with a dark brown patina. Hollow sand-cast. Mounted on a cube of AIgcrian onyx. Bequeathed by Indy Theobald, 1938. Rodin received the commission to make a public statue of Balzac from the Societe dcs Gens de Lettrcs in July 1891 and undertook to complete the work in eighteen months. The Society had opened a subscription in November 1883 and commissioned Henri Chapu to produce a group in 1889. Chapu had died in 1891 before his work was complete and the commission was transferred to Rodin through the influence of Emilc Zola in controversial circumstances. The Society nearly withdrew the commission in 1894 out of impatience with Rodin's delays and, when the finished marble was exhibited in May 1898 at the Salon of the Societ€ Nationale, the Society denounced it and transfcrrcd the commission to Alcxandre Falguiere. Rodin's admirers quickly found the money to purchase the work, but the artist decided to keep it for himself. The first bronze cast was made in 1930 for the Koninklyk Museum voor Schone Kunstcn, Antwerp, and in July 1939 another was crcctcd at the Confluence of the Boulevards Raspail and Montparnasse in Paris by a belatedly rcpcntant Societe dcs Gens de Lettres. Some of the numerous sketches for Balzac's head had however been cast in bronze in the artist's lifctimc (those in the Kunstmuseum Athcnaeum at Helsinki and in the Cleveland Museum of Art were purchased in 1901). The bronze in the Ashmolean Museum reproduces one of the boldest of the sketch heads dated to 1897 when the artist's last and most dynamic ideas for the monument wcrc being'devclopcd. It is a variant of a head which exists in stoneware (examples are in the Mus6e .Rbdin, the Mus6e du Petit Palais, and the Musec du Conservatoire des Arts et M€tiers, all in Paris) and is c.Ioscly rclatcd to a colossal head of the novelist. Five casts of this type (including the Ashmolcan's, which is illustrated) are catalogucd in John L. Tancock, 777c Sc#/Pf"rc a/A"g#f}G Ro¢¢." (Philadelphia, 1979), 457, and one of these (in the William Haycs Ackland Memorial Art Center, University of North Carolina, Chapel Hill) was purchased from the artist. At least one other of this type has once appeared on the market (Christie's, New York, 13 February 1986, lot 17)-and this, like the cast in the Hirschom Museum and Sculpture Garden, is marked as cast by Alexis Rudier. Tancock also provides a good account of the evolution of the Balzac statue and all the by-products of the commission (ibid. 42545). Among other accounts that by Cecilc Goldscheider, `I,a Genese d'une ceuvrc: I.c Balzac de Rodin', Rc77#G def ¢rff, 2 (1952), 3744, remains fundamental. Probably foundry of Georges RUDIER After Augustc RODIN (1840-1917)

334 and 335. Pair of candlesticks decorated with terminal figures of the four Seasons

@@PROCESS@@
Bronze, fire-gilt. The Slding is scratched and won to reveal the yellow metal especially on more salient ornaments, such as the breasts of the female tcms. The oridnal very high burnishing and warm colour of the Slding survives under the knob where the base rises into the balustcr. The urn top of one candelabrum has a hole drilled in its side. (The arms were engraved prcwious to their apphcation.) The nature of the cracks which could not have been acceptable and the old bolts attaching the shot ornament suggest that the latter was, however, apphied bcforc the candlesticks were put to use. The candlesticks are both composed as follows. Mallett notes that it came from the `Harold Davis Collection 1933'. Malett described these candlesticks as French of about 1740. The design is French but of an earlier period. The shape of the uns and their gadrooning; the pendant ornaments on the three female personifications and the strapwork with solid tassels on the side of the urns; the diaper ornament with quatrefoils on the knob-ll this is characteristic of French design in the late seventeenth century. The diaper work, for instance, was much favoured by J. Hardouin-Mansart-s in the marble vases carved to his design by Buirette for the gardens at Vcrsaillcs (1685-93) or in the stucco cornice he designed for the Salon de l'ail de Bceuf at Vcrsaillcs (1701). The strapwork with sohd ben-shaped tassels (or `campanes'), here found at the top of the candlestick, will bc found on a large scale in lead on the roof of the chapel at Versailles (1705-7) and in a design for a magnificent perfume burner (Tessin Collection National Museum, Stockholm) as well as in the ffinges of furniture. Terms of the Seasons were also popular at Versailles and tern figLires of similar character arc to bc found on clock cases by Boulle. Although well designed and cxccuted, one suspects that these candlesticks are a modified reduction of more splendid and accomplished work. The three feminine Seasons are distinguished by their attributes (Spring flowers, Summer wine, and Autumn com) in their hair and on their piers, but not at all by their looks or attitudes. Around 1700 three types of crucifix had become cstablishcd in popularity: Christ dying with his head tumcd up to Heaven and his arms upstretched in the form of a Y (which made it sometimes possible to fashion the body from a single piccc of ivory); Christ dead, sagting, his eyes closed (this is relatively rare but there were very famous examples including one made by Duquesnoy for Pope Urban VIII which was much copied); Christ alive looking with compassion at those at the foot of the Cross or at the thief executed beside him. This crucifix belongs to the latter category. In each type by that date the nudity of Christ was almost invariably covered by the `perizonium'. The question of whcthcr Christ's feet were fixed to the Cross scparatcly with two nals, as hcrc, or together with one nail, was much discussed. The former solution was more popular with artists in France and Spain than in Italy-where, however, Algardi in his much-acclaimed and imitated Crucifixions also preferred to represent the feet in this way. (For this queshon see E. Mdic, L'Art religieun de la fin dtt Xvle si6cle et du XVIIIe siicle (Pins, \951), 270-1). @@PROCESS@@
Ivory discoloured with dirt in the intricacies of the relief, but naturally yellow in parts of the back of the flask and on the back of the nozzle. The spring valve and thumb-piece, both probably originally of silver, are missing from the nozzle. The nozzle, the scrolled suspension ring, and the studs are all separate pieces. So too is the cusped rose in the centre of the back of the flask and the roundel in the front (and lid) of the flask. The main pall:s of the front and back of the flask are carved out of a single piece each. The front (and lid) has cracked in two lines. One crack passes through the ankle of the man crouched with a gun beside a cow and through the trunk of the tree behind him, continuing through the lower legs of the man confronting the ostrich. This crack is also apparent on the rim of the lid behind the nozzle. Old glue is visible in the interior of the flask. Particularly ambitious works by him are in the Bayerisches National-museum, Munich-holy water vessel (R. 4482), goblet (R. 4759), `prunkschtissel' (R. 4908), and tankard (R. 6596). Theuerkauff speculates that the artist is likely to have been born between 1630 and 1640. The latest date on any of the works bearing his monogram to survive is 1679 (the relief of the S¢c7?/;ce a/Jf¢¢c in the Victoria and Albert Museum), but a knife handle once in Schloss Ambras, Innsbruck, was dated 1680. Nearly all works signed `B.G.' which have any provenance come from Vienna. Powder-fias-ks decorated with hunting scenes were not uncommon in the seventeenth century. There are three in the Victoria and Albert Museum, 2161, 2199, and 2220-1855 (all from the collection of Ralph Bernal), of which the last mentioned retains all its origival fittings. There are two other known powder-flasks of comparable design signed `B.G.'. One is in the Armoury of the Kunsthistorisches Museum, Vienna (D.149, no. 18, and fig. t in the catalogue by Theucrkauff, of 1966, cited above). It is rather smaller (10.8 cms. in diameter), has similar lobed and cusped decoration on the back, and is signed `B.G.' in the same manner and in the same place. In it the central roundel represents a resting stag rather than Actaeon splashed by Diana, but it is of similar virtuosity with branches and antlers undercut and many minute overlapping elements. As in the Ashmolean powder-flask different methods of hunting are represented in reliefs divided by trees; the latter, however, are relatively larger. The spearing of a wild boar is an episode found on both, but the scenes are otherwise different. The reliefs on the Kunsthistorisches Museum's flask are closer in the style to the scenes of rural life framed by trees on the oval platter in the same Museum (4470; ibid., no.15, fig. p). The hunting scenes on the tankard in Munich arc generally of a similar kind. The other similar powder-flask signed `B.G.' is in the Civic Museum, Kassel (8. VI. 142; ibid., no.19, fig. u). In this case the central relief is of Diam resting with her hounds and the surrounding reliefs illustrate hunting episodes closer in character and scale to those in the Ashmolean's flask than were those on the flask in Vienna. The subject~matter is also closer-here too a man crouches behind a ruminant hoping to surprise birds, an ostrich is taken with forked sticks, a wild boar is speared, and a stag is pursued-but the compositions are not the same and the effect of the Ashmolean's relief is more crowded. Thcuerkauff mentions other powder-flasks decorated in this manner in the Gugivi Collection, Florence, and the Musikhistoriska Museet, Stockholm, and has catalogued two others as from the `workshop or possible circle' of the Master one of which is in the armoury of the Kunsthistorisches Museum, D.150, and the other is in the . The proportions of the figure are immediately suggestive of northern Europe-this is a sister of the nude woman in a bathhouse depicted in prints by Dtirer and his followers and of the L#c7c££¢f attributed to such sculptors as Joachim Forster or Conrat Meit (see the bronze and boxwood figures in the Kunsthistorischcs Museum, Vienna, 5972 and 303- 7610 respectively) with their plump buttocks and behies, but the work is inspired by the antique statue of the C4}//a.¢%v¢.¢7¢ Vc79#J which is first recorded for certain in the Farnese Palace in Rome in 1594 and is likely to have been excavated in Rome in the middle of the sixteenth century (see F. Haskell and N. Penny, T¢J£G ¢7¢d £4e A7¢f¢.¢#G (New Haven, Conn., and I.ondon,1981), 316-18). This suggests a northern artist who had travelled to Italy in the second half of the century. Furthermore, the anatomical elasticity (not only of the body but of the fingers) and the elaborate coiffure are highly influenced both by the sculpture of Giambologna and also by the court art of the Emperor Rudolf 11 and, in particular, the erotic mythologies painted by Bartholomaus Spranger (1546- |611). What little we know of Mont tallies very well with this. And this bronze has been widely accepted as by him. I do not believe that it is, but it seems appropriate to review the case in detail. Mont was described by our principal source concerning him, Karel van Mander's biography of spranger, as a youngish man in about 1575 in which year he entered the service of the Emperor Maximilian 11 in Vienna, together with Spranger, at the recommendation of Giambologna whose pupil he had been in Italy. In the Fasanengarten Mont cxccutcd figures in stucco and Spranger painted frescoes and the two artists worked together also on the triumphal arch erected in the Farmer's market for the entry into Vienna of Maximilian's successor, Rudolf 11, in 1576-Mont was responsible for the architecture and the sculpture, of plaster and terracotta painted white, representing Justice and Prudence, Fortitude and Temperance, escorting Maximilian and Rudolf and a Pegasus larger than life. Spranger painted allegorical reliefs in imitation of bronze (in this undertaking Mont would seem to have been the senior partner). There are payments in 1571 to a Hans Mont in the court archives in Vienna which suggest that van Mander might have been mistaken concerning the date at which the sculptor first commenced work there. In 1572 a `pictor' called Mont was recommended by the Emperor Maximilian to the duke of Mantua-the term `pictor' was sometimes used for sculptors and this suggests that Mont was by then in Italy. A Giovanni Mont was paid for working under Giambologna on the Fountain of Oceanus in the Boboli Gardens in Florence between 7 June and 20 September of 1572. The name Hans Mont appears, above that of a Hans Smelris van Brusel which is dated 1574, among the graffiti in the subterranean ruins of Nero's Golden House in Rome. In October 1574 a `Juande Mont' was receiving a salary from the court in Vienna, backdated to July 1573. After 1576 Mont is said by van Mander to have travelled to Prague with the new emperor perhaps to work on the elaborate catafalque for Maximilian and on adapting the Castle. Van Mander claims that he found little employment in the new court and took leave, `forgetting' to return, and ended up in Turkey, but a different account is provided by the diary of a merchant from Ulm, Hans Kraffi, who visited Spranger in Prague in 1584 and was told by him that Mont had been blinded in his left eye by a ball when watching a game of raquets at court on account of which he had given up his sculpture and concentrated on architecture, working at Ulm, but also travelling to Italy. (The above is taken from L. 0. Larsson, `Hans Mont van Gent', Ko79f£¢¢.rfo7.;4 f¢.dr47.¢/f (1967),I-12, and id., `Bildhauerkunst und Plastik am Hofe Kaiser Rudolfs 11', Lc¢.dr 4%7¢fz:¢¢.j}o7`¢.fc47 /.¢¢7.4ocfe (1982), i. 214-19; but for the refcrencc to the Golden House see F. Weegc, `Das Goldene Haus des Nero (Neue Funde und Forschungen)' , /¢4/7r4#c¢ dcJ ji{¢¢.Jc7./¢.c4 deutschen archdologischen Instituts, 28 (L9L3), ±47 , fig. 6) . With the exception of two drawings, one in a private collection in Amsterdam and the other in the Uffizi (P7t¢g elm 1600: Kunst tend Kultttr am Hofe Rttdolft 11 (Fissen. 1988), nos. 232 and 233), both anciently inscribed as by him, there are no surviving works which can confidently be associated with Mont. Without knowledge of Parkcr's attribution of the Ashmolean bronze to Mont, Larsson proposed, tentatively, that an almost life-size bronze group of Mars and Venus seated, embracing, in a complex pose very close in composition to the work of sprangcr, may be by him. The group is now in the Royal Park at Drottingholm and must have been looted from Prague together with the bronze groups by Adriaen de Vries, but it is both inferior as a cast and less vital and skilful in modelling than the latter. Very close in composition but smaller in size (37 cms. high) is a highly finished marble group in the Gemaldegalerie, Prague. This too Larsson proposed as by Mont. By 1982 (see the references above) Larsson knew of parker's attribution and accepted it, following the compilers of the G;¢7#4o/og7¢¢ exhibition in Vienna in 1978 (see the entry by M. Leithe- Jasper for the German version of the catalogue). Mont's skill as a worker in stucco has also suggested to I . Krvc:Alov& (in Die Kunst der Renaissance and de§ A4l¢7¢¢.Gr;r7„#f ¢.7¢ Bo¢77¢e79 (I'rague, 1979), 104) that he might be the author of the spectacular decorations of about 1585 in this material in the Moravian castle of BUEovicc and this attribution has also received cautious support (see P7.¢g #ow 1600, cited above, fig. 3). Crucial to any assessments of these attributions is the question of whether Mont worked in bronze: contemporaries only specify stone, stucco, and terracotta; PhiLipp Halnhofer in his journal of 1617 records six marble and bronze statues by `Giovan di Mente' and `Giovan Bologna' in the possession of the elector of Saxony (`Reise-Tagebuch', B¢/f;j.c¢c Sf#d¢.c72 (Stettin,1834), ii.134-ited by Larsson,1967, n.15) but it is not clear even here that the works by Mont were of bronze. If Mont's work was cast by others it could vary greatly in finish. Certainly the large bronze at Drottingholm is a poor cast, inadequately chiselled, whereas the Ashmolean's bronze is of superlative quality, superbly finished. The problem does not end there, however, for the Drottingholm bronze is a fascinating but languid and awkward composition, with the figures loosely articulated, which the Ashmolean's VG7¢ac is not. The fulings of the Drottingholm group are shared by the smaller marble in Prague. This marble is in fact reproduced in a small bronze and there is another, very similar, small bronze group of 44¢7.f ¢7O¢ Vc7¢#J in the J. Paul Getty Museum (85.SB.75, 54 cms. high). The latter is in turn exceedingly close to a bronze formerly in the collection of Kenneth Clark (lot 74, Sotheby's, London, 10 December 1987) which is derived from Giambologna's Vc7¢#r U7`¢7¢¢.¢ but with a calendar, sundial, and other accessories (some now lost)- the coiffiire and facial expression of the female figures are as close as the colour of the metal. In the Ashmolean bronze, which is comparable in size with these three pieces, the hair is far less tightly composed, the features are far fuller, the colour (which appears to be original) entirely different, and we are more conscious even than when comparing the Ashmolean's VG7¢#f with the Drottingholm and Prague groups of the greater coherence, energy, and plasticity of the modelling. I owe to Volker Krahn the idea that the Vc7?#J might be the work of a more famous sculptor than Mont-Hubcrt Gcrhard. The treatment of hands and hair, also the fleshy plumpness and torsion, is parallelcd in the Venus of the colossal group of A4l¢7if, Vc„#f, ¢7¢d C#p¢.d made for Graf Hans Fuggcr bctwcen 1584 and 1590 (Baycrischcs Nationalmuseum, Munich, R. 6986; H. Pierced miniature carvings of episodes of the Passion in an identical style, with gaunt, stiffly articulated figures, idiosyncratic late Gothic architecture, and tinted soil or rockwork appear against a background of humming-bird feather mosaic (which was a Mexican speciality) in an cnamclled triptych in the Schatzkammer, Munich, in a silver-mountcd triptych in the Victoria and Albert Museum (226- 1866), in a wooden triptych in the Licbicghaus, Frankfurt (inv. no. 615),in a triptych in the Carrand collection in the Bargello, Florence (inv. no. 1035c), in the knop and foot of a rock crystal chalice (with silver gilt mounts bearing a Mexican mark) in the I.os Angeles County Museum, and in lantcrn-shaped rock crystal jewcllcd pendants in the British Museum, the Waltcrs Art Gallery, Baltimore, the Phyllis Phillips Collection (lot 453, Christic's, I.ondon,13 December 1989), and clscwhcrc. Most other versions are of the figure with right hand raised (an cxccption is a very poor cast on the art market in 1965). And no other versions arc as richly tcxtured. It is the appearance of the younger figures in the silver set, and in particular the face of the younger man, that points to the work of a northern goldsmith. No small bronze figures and very few silver ones surviving from the sixteenth century possess a finish as exquisite as these bronze oncs and none of comparable workmanship achieve such remarkable expression in the faces and in the gestures, restrained though thcsc arc. It is often assumed that goldsmiths in this period worked in silver and in bronze but it is exceedingly unusual for examples to exist in both materials which arc so similar that they are likely to have been finished by the same hand as is the case here.

Registered on 4 November 1963. Ian Robcrtson publishing this sculpture in the A»»#¢/ RcPo7¢ for 1963 (p. 52) described it as `cightccnth-century' and `in all probability Italian, although a French oriSn cannot entirely bc ruled out', and commended the `high degree of skill and, in its detail, an equally high degrcc of sensitivity'. The archaic convention of disparity in scale in a group of this kind would seem surprising in the eighteenth century but was revived in the last century for church art. The group is in fact reminiscent of many carvings made bctwccn 1840 and 1900 all over northern Europe in which some aspects of Gothic style and some medieval conventions were skilfully adapted to a work which rctaincd much modern sentiment and academic skill. Charles Gcerts of Louvain was particularly prolific and successful in this mode. An attribution to him was tcntativcly suggested to me by Malcolm Baker. The back face of the plinth origivally carried a signature, as Robcrtson did not notice (and perhaps was not meant to notice). This face has been shaved down and scratched so that this is no longer dcciphcrablc but it commcnccs with a capital 8 or P, continues after a space possibly with `luis', and then a large `h' with a curled tail, three or four smaller lcttcrs, and a double `SS'; then there is the vestige of an `Ft.' and the numerals of a date. All that is certain is that the eccentric style of lettering irregularly algncd and with letters of uneven size is typical of that used by French and Flemish artists around 1880.
